    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="06061391cd634843b710fac587b527d4">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'a2b32f2c-4271-4fa5-a59a-8f273f30dff1'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d148811055" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Layered oxides, the most commercially promising cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), are extensively investigated due to their low cost, abundant raw materials, flexible structure, and high capacity. However, although the “rotten” transition metal (TM) migration phenomenon in layered oxide cathode materials leads to unfavorable structure reconstruction, sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion and subsequent performance degradation, TM migration has not been systematically summarized and intensively discussed. Herein, a comprehensive insight into the recent advances of TM migration in layered SIB cathodes is provided, aiming to realize the “magical” utilization of TM migration. First, the negative effects of TM migration on crystal structure and electrochemical properties are discussed. Then, the origin of TM migration is intensively analyzed, and three main mechanisms of the migration are presented, including Cr<sup>4+</sup>–Cr<sup>4+</sup>–Cr<sup>4+</sup> triplets, Jahn–Teller Fe<sup>4+</sup> and Na-free layers. Additionally, the latest research findings on exploring the origin are fully discussed. Finally, the suppression and utilization of TM migration are discussed, and the further exploration of TM migration and the future directions of suppressing/utilizing TM migration are outlooked. It is believed that this perspective will provide guidelines for the design of high-performance layered oxide cathodes involving TM migration by turning the “rotten” into the “magical.”</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202313234-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202313234-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>To fully utilize green energy (e.g., solar, wind, and hydropower), large-scale electrochemical energy storage devices with geographical independence, timely response and high energy density are urgently needed.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0001_R_d148811046e391" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">1</a>-<a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0004_R_d148811046e391" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nature">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although initial success of utilizing lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) in energy storage is apparent, the future development of LIBs in large-scale energy storage is limited by the poor reserve and high cost of lithium raw materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0005_R_d148811046e396" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nature">5</a>-<a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0008_R_d148811046e396" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nat. Energy">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Fortunately, benefiting from the rich reserve and cost-effectiveness of sodium raw materials, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) show the potential to replace LIBs for large-scale energy storage. As the cathode accounts for one-third of the cost and determines the electrochemical performance of the whole battery for SIBs, cathodes are one of the most critical battery components and have been extensively investigated.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0009_R_d148811046e401" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater.">9</a>-<a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0012_R_d148811046e401" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Energy Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>To date, Prussian blue analogues, polyanionic compounds, and layered oxides are the most widely investigated cathode materials (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202313234-fig-0001">1</a>) that are likely to realize large-scale commercial applications, where layered oxides are an ideal choice for SIB cathode materials due to their high specific capacity, abundant raw materials, flexible structure, and uncomplicated preparation process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0013_R_d148811046e414" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Environ. Sci.">13</a>-<a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0016_R_d148811046e414" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, layered oxide cathodes undergo unfavorable transition metal (TM) interlayer migration under Na-deinsertion states, which will induce unfavorable structural rearrangement and ion diffusion blockage, leading to poor initial efficiency and capacity degradation. Over the past decade, many researches have been devoted to reveal the origin of TM migration and develop novel strategies to address TM migration to realize high-performance layered oxide cathodes involving TM migration, while TM migration has not been systematically summarized and intensively discussed. Therefore, a timely and comprehensive overview of the advance of layered oxide cathodes involving TM migration is urgently required to provide guidelines for further development and stimulate more interested researches in the field.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202313234-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/229c7a6b-90a8-4807-9802-c5acfaca758c/adfm202313234-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/229c7a6b-90a8-4807-9802-c5acfaca758c/adfm202313234-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6e4d3354-1187-420b-a49d-ee0322eb8148/adfm202313234-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/229c7a6b-90a8-4807-9802-c5acfaca758c/adfm202313234-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202313234-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202313234" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The publications on Prussian blue analogues, polyanionic compounds, and layered oxides as cathode materials for SIBs from 2018 to 2023, data: October 2023, database: Web of Science Core Collection, search strategies: TOPIC&nbsp;=&nbsp;(“sodium ion batter*” OR “sodium batter*” OR “Na ion batter*” OR “Na batter*”) AND TOPIC = (“cathode*” OR “positive electrode*”) AND TOPIC = (“polyanionic”) for polyanionic compounds, TOPIC&nbsp;= (“sodium ion batter*” OR “sodium batter*” OR “Na ion batter*” OR “Na batter*”) AND TOPIC&nbsp;= (“cathode*” OR “positive electrode*”) AND TOPIC&nbsp;= (“Prussian blue *”) for Prussian blue analogues, TOPIC = (“sodium ion batter*” OR “sodium batter*” OR “Na ion batter*” OR “Na batter*”) AND TOPIC&nbsp;= (“cathode*” OR “positive electrode*”) AND TOPIC = (“layered oxide*”) for layered oxides.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Herein, we present a comprehensive, systematic, and in-depth insight into recent advances of TM migration in layered oxide cathodes of SIBs and plot the relationship between electrochemical performance and TM migration in layered oxide cathodes of SIBs (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202313234-fig-0002">2</a>), where the “rotten” TM migration associated with structural and performance degradation can be regulated by suppressing the formation of Na-free layers, mitigating Fe<sup>4+</sup> Jahn–Teller effect, and disrupting Cr<sup>4+</sup>–Cr<sup>4+</sup>–Cr<sup>4+</sup> triplets to realize “magical” stable/reversible structure and excellent electrochemical performance. Initially, the typical TM migration in typical Cr/Fe-based and other layered oxide cathodes, fundamental characterizations, and corresponding electrochemical characteristics are discussed. Then, the specific migration mechanisms (e.g., Cr<sup>4+</sup>–Cr<sup>4+</sup>–Cr<sup>4+</sup> triplets, Jahn–Teller Fe<sup>4+</sup> and Na-free layers) are summarized and intensively analyzed, and then the suppression and even utilization of TM migration in layered oxide cathodes to realize excellent electrochemical performance is also discussed. Finally, the challenges of the currently proposed TM migration mechanisms are noted, and further directions of layered oxide cathodes involving TM migration are outlooked.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202313234-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/09ed7d25-248f-4080-bcbe-c80fb92c157b/adfm202313234-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/09ed7d25-248f-4080-bcbe-c80fb92c157b/adfm202313234-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3876ff58-97d0-43be-ab30-67c9f6ce0ee0/adfm202313234-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/09ed7d25-248f-4080-bcbe-c80fb92c157b/adfm202313234-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202313234-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202313234" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The relationship between electrochemical performance and TM migration in layered oxide cathodes of SIBs. The “rotten” TM migration induces unfavorable structural rearrangement, ion diffusion blockage and low initial efficiency, and the accumulated TM migration during repeated charge and discharge leads to continued electrochemical performance degradation. The “rotten” TM migration can be regulated and even utilized by suppressing the formation of Na-free layers, mitigating the Fe<sup>4+</sup> Jahn–Teller effect and disrupting the Cr<sup>4+</sup>–Cr<sup>4+</sup>–Cr<sup>4+</sup> triplets to realize “magical” stable/reversible structure, low lattice variation, high initial efficiency, and ultimately excellent electrochemical performance for layered oxide cathodes of SIBs.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202313234-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202313234-sec-0020-title">2 TM Migration</h2>
         
         <p>TM migration was initially investigated in layered oxide cathodes of LIBs, such as the typical Li/Ni disordering.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0017_R_d148811046e500" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Acc. Chem. Res.">17</a>-<a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0020_R_d148811046e500" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Chem. Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Particularly, the unfavorable TM migration in LIBs is usually attributed to the similar Li<sup>+</sup> (0.76&nbsp;Å) and Ni<sup>2+</sup> (0.69&nbsp;Å) ionic radius. However, since the ionic radius of Na<sup>+</sup> (1.02&nbsp;Å) is much larger than that of the TM ions, the TM migration in SIBs is different from that in LIBs. As illustrated in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202313234-fig-0003">3</a>, based on the theoretical calculations, Ti, Cr, and Fe in TM sites of layered oxide cathodes are prone to migrate from the TM sites to the sodium sites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0021_R_d148811046e517" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As the scope of our discussion here is the cathode, we will not explore Ti below while Cr and Fe will be thorough discussed.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202313234-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/184f2b86-d942-4c0f-9175-50e2a1fe25a0/adfm202313234-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/184f2b86-d942-4c0f-9175-50e2a1fe25a0/adfm202313234-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c0fffe34-449d-4f37-b008-fb264ea5a71a/adfm202313234-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/184f2b86-d942-4c0f-9175-50e2a1fe25a0/adfm202313234-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202313234-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202313234" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Energy difference of spinel-type NaTM<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and layered Na<sub>0.5</sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> (TM&nbsp;=&nbsp;Ti, V, Cr, Mn, Fe, Co, and Ni). For cathodes, Cr and Fe are prone to migrate from TM sites to Na sites. Reproduced with permission.<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0021" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0021_R_d148811046e550" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Copyright 2012, Royal Society of Chemistry.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202313234-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202313234-sec-0030-title">2.1 TM Migration in Cr-Based Layered Oxide Cathodes</h3>
            
            <p>For TM migration, Cr-based layered oxide cathodes have been the most extensively investigated.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0022_R_d148811046e564" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Phys. Chem. Lett.">22</a>-<a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0024_R_d148811046e564" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Chem. Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> NaCrO<sub>2</sub> is free of TM migration and demonstrates an excellent structural and electrochemical reversibility when the cut-off voltage is below 3.6&nbsp;V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0025_R_d148811046e571" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Electrochem. Commun.">25</a>-<a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0027_R_d148811046e571" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Electrochem. Commun.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A carbon-coated NaCrO<sub>2</sub> cathode was investigated by Myung et&nbsp;al. and tested in 2.0–3.6&nbsp;V, and an excellent electrochemical performance (an ultra-high rate capability of 150C and a long cycle stability of 300&nbsp;cycles) was obtained.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0028_R_d148811046e579" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Energy Environ. Sci.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> When the cut-off voltage is above 3.6&nbsp;V, TM migration will be triggered, leading to structural damage and irreversible capacity. The irreversible capacities of NaCrO<sub>2</sub> in different voltage windows were systematically explored by Komaba et&nbsp;al., and corresponding structural evolution was also evaluated.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0029_R_d148811046e586" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Phys. Chem. C">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In 2.5–3.6&nbsp;V, a reversible capacity of 110&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> was obtained for NaCrO<sub>2</sub> with almost no irreversible capacity. As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202313234-fig-0004">4a</a>, a reversible capacity of 118&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> was obtained with an irreversible capacity of about 8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> in 2.5–3.7&nbsp;V. When the cutoff voltage was 3.8&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202313234-fig-0004">4a</a>), the reversible capacity decreased to 90&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, and an ultra-small reversible capacity of 9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> was obtained in 2.5–4.5&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202313234-fig-0004">4a</a>) and corresponding charge capacity was up to about 235&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (corresponding to 0.94&nbsp;Na deinsertion). Ex situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) results (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202313234-fig-0004">4b</a>) revealed that the high irreversible capacity was associated with the structural evolution under high sodium deinsertion states. The fully charged Na<sub>0.06</sub>CrO<sub>2</sub> exhibited severe TM migration from TM layers to Na layers. Specifically, the migrated Cr in sodium layers is found in both CrO<sub>6</sub> octahedral and CrO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedral sites, and the total migrated Cr can up to 30%. Subsequently, the structural evolution of NaCrO<sub>2</sub> was further explored by Ceder et&nbsp;al. through in situ XRD and electron diffraction techniques.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0030_R_d148811046e634" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Chem. Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> No Cr migration was found in Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>CrO<sub>2</sub> with <i>x</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;1–0.4. Layered P′3-type Na<sub>0.4</sub>CrO<sub>2</sub> phase would convert to a layered O3-type Na<i><sub>δ</sub></i>CrO<sub>2</sub> intermediate phase and then to a final rock-salt-like CrO<sub>2</sub> phase.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202313234-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/61bacf19-f9c9-457f-a843-b7ff86614eb6/adfm202313234-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/61bacf19-f9c9-457f-a843-b7ff86614eb6/adfm202313234-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4b0297b6-4627-41e5-b7fa-2f4a497e0014/adfm202313234-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/61bacf19-f9c9-457f-a843-b7ff86614eb6/adfm202313234-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202313234-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202313234" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Initial charge and discharge profiles of NaCrO<sub>2</sub> in 2.5–3.7&nbsp;V (0.5&nbsp;Na deinsertion), 2.5–3.8&nbsp;V (0.7&nbsp;Na deinsertion), and 2.5–4.5&nbsp;V (0.94&nbsp;Na deinsertion). b) Ex situ XRD patterns of charged NaCrO<sub>2</sub> (3.7 and 4.5&nbsp;V) and corresponding simulated XRD patterns of fully charged Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>CrO<sub>2</sub> (4.5&nbsp;V) with different Cr migrating to CrO<sub>6</sub> octahedral and CrO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedral sites in sodium layers. Reproduced with permission.<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0029" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0029_R_d148811046e692" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Phys. Chem. C">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Copyright 2014, American Chemical Society.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202313234-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202313234-sec-0040-title">2.2 TM Migration in Fe-Based Layered Oxide Cathodes</h3>
            
            <p>NaFeO<sub>2</sub> is another kind of layered oxide cathode suffering from serious TM migration and subsequent capacity irreversibility. The capacity reversibility of NaFeO<sub>2</sub> is also highly dependent on the charge cut-off voltage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0031_R_d148811046e710" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Sci.">31</a>-<a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0033_R_d148811046e710" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The reversible capacity and corresponding structural evolution of NaFeO<sub>2</sub> in different voltage windows was systematically investigated by Komaba et&nbsp;al., and NaFeO<sub>2</sub> showed an excellent reversibility when the cut-off voltage was below 3.5&nbsp;V (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202313234-fig-0005">5a</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0034_R_d148811046e726" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Electrochemistry">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the reversibility would gradually decrease with the cut-off voltage increasing when the cut-off voltage was above 3.5&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202313234-fig-0005">5a</a>). Ex situ XRD results (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202313234-fig-0005">5b</a>) of NaFeO<sub>2</sub> after initial charge and discharge with different cut-off voltages suggested that the capacity reversibility was associated with the crystal structure evolution. When the cut-off voltage was set to 3.4&nbsp;V, the crystal structure remained intact throughout the initial charge and discharge processes. When the cut-off voltage was set to 4.0 and 4.5&nbsp;V, serious irreversible structural variation was found, and the irreversible structural variation could be mainly attributed to Fe interlayer migration during charging, leading to the irreversible capacity. The migration behavior of Fe in NaFeO<sub>2</sub>, with a cut-off voltage of 4.0&nbsp;V, was further explored by Saurel et&nbsp;al. through in situ/operando XRD, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy and potential intermittent titration technique.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0035_R_d148811046e742" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Mater. Chem. A">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The migration was found to be related with both the (00l) peak intensity and the intralayer/interlayer distance, and about 40% Fe was found to migrate from TM layers to sodium layers through quantitatively analyzing the (003) peak intensity when the cut-off voltage was set to 4.0&nbsp;V. Interestingly, part of the 40% migrated Fe in sodium layers would re-migrate from the sodium layers to TM layers in subsequent discharge process, and the sodium diffusion coefficient was not affected. The migrated Fe of Na<sub>x</sub>FeO<sub>2</sub> (charged to 4.0&nbsp;V) in sodium layers was first observed at atomic scale by Wang et&nbsp;al. through high angle annular dark field-scanning transmission electron microscopy (STEM), and the chemical state of the migrated Fe was also confirmed by electron energy loss spectroscopy (EELS), shown in (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202313234-fig-0005">5c,d</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0036_R_d148811046e755" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Nano Energy">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> EELS analysis indicated that the Fe in TM layers was much higher than Fe<sup>3+</sup> and the migrated Fe in Na layers was slightly higher than Fe<sup>3+</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202313234-fig-0005">5e</a>).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202313234-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9434a18f-f31a-45bf-86cd-6c69d2945920/adfm202313234-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/9434a18f-f31a-45bf-86cd-6c69d2945920/adfm202313234-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ff9aa428-fb10-4788-860b-004a662fb4e0/adfm202313234-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9434a18f-f31a-45bf-86cd-6c69d2945920/adfm202313234-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202313234-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202313234" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>

                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Initial charge and discharge profiles of NaFeO<sub>2</sub> with the cut-off voltages of 3.4, 3.5, 3.6, 4.0, and 4.5&nbsp;V. b) Ex situ XRD patterns of NaFeO<sub>2</sub> after initial charge and discharge with the cut-off voltages of 3.4, 4.0, and 4.5&nbsp;V. Reproduced with permission.<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0034" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0034_R_d148811046e792" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Electrochemistry">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Copyright 2012, Electrochemical Soc Japan. c) High-angle annular dark-field image of fully charged NaFeO<sub>2</sub> and d) corresponding line profile of the red box region, the white and pink arrows point to Na and Fe layers, respectively. e) EELS spectra of Fe in Na and Fe layers.                   </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202313234-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202313234-sec-0050-title">2.3 TM Migration in other Layered Oxide Cathodes</h3>
            
            <p>Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>VO<sub>2</sub> can undergo reversible structure evolution in the range of <i>x</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;1–0.5, and two single phases of Na<sub>1/2</sub>VO<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>2/3</sub>VO<sub>2</sub> can be observed. Delmas et&nbsp;al. reported that a reversible capacity of 126.4&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (corresponding to 0.5&nbsp;Na<sup>+</sup> (de)insertion) was obtained in NaVO<sub>2</sub>, while serious irreversible capacity would appear when more than 0.5&nbsp;Na<sup>+</sup> was deinserted.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0037_R_d148811046e839" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Electrochem. Solid-State Lett.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The irreversible capacity was attributed to the unfavorable V migration from TM layers to sodium layers. In addition to the common Cr, Fe, and V, Ni,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0038_R_d148811046e844" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Sci. Bull.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Li,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0039_R_d148811046e849" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Nature">39</a>, <a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0040_R_d148811046e849" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Nat. Mater.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Mg,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0041_R_d148811046e855" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Joule">41</a>, <a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0042_R_d148811046e855" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Adv. Mater.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and Zn<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0043_R_d148811046e860" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Nat. Commun.">43</a>, <a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0044_R_d148811046e860" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Adv. Mater.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> are also found to migrate from TM layers to sodium layers, while these migrations show a weak influence on irreversible capacity.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202313234-sec-0060">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202313234-sec-0060-title">3 Origin and Suppression of TM Migration</h2>
         
         <p>The TM migration is along the octahedron (TM layer)—tetrahedron (Na layer)—octahedron (Na layer) pathway.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0021_R_d148811046e875" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In Cr-based layered oxide cathodes, it is well accepted the charge disproportionation of Cr<sup>4+</sup> (3Cr<sup>4+</sup>&nbsp;→&nbsp;2Cr<sup>3+</sup>&nbsp;+&nbsp;Cr<sup>6+</sup>) triggers the Cr migration during charge because the Cr<sup>6+</sup> with small ionic radius prefers to migrate from the octahedral site in TM layers to the tetrahedral site in sodium layers, as shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202313234-fig-0006">6a</a>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0030_R_d148811046e897" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Chem. Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To suppress the charge disproportionation of Cr<sup>4+</sup>, it is key to avoid the formation of Cr<sup>4+</sup>–Cr<sup>4+</sup>–Cr<sup>4+</sup> triplets in TM layers. Based on this opinion, substitution is accepted to be an ideal strategy to suppress Cr migration. Therefore, several Cr-based layered oxide cathodes, such as Na<sub>0.6</sub>Cr<sub>0.6</sub>Ti<sub>0.4</sub>O<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0045_R_d148811046e920" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Nat. Commun.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> NaCr<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0046_R_d148811046e934" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - J. Mater. Chem. A">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sub>0.66</sub>Cr<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Ti<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0047_R_d148811046e950" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> NaCr<sub>0.8</sub>Mn<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0048_R_d148811046e962" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - J. Power Sources">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and NaCr<sub>1/4</sub>Fe<sub>1/4</sub>Ni<sub>1/4</sub>Ti<sub>1/4</sub>O<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0049_R_d148811046e978" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Energy Storage Mater.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> demonstrate excellent structure and capacity reversibility.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202313234-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7b8452e8-b9fa-4235-a806-02fa57fc6a5b/adfm202313234-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/7b8452e8-b9fa-4235-a806-02fa57fc6a5b/adfm202313234-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/49993d1a-be47-4f0c-a768-68c201c5e22a/adfm202313234-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7b8452e8-b9fa-4235-a806-02fa57fc6a5b/adfm202313234-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202313234-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202313234" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The Cr<sup>4+</sup>–Cr<sup>4+</sup>–Cr<sup>4+</sup> triplets, the charge disproportionation of Cr<sup>4+</sup> (3Cr<sup>4+</sup>&nbsp;→&nbsp;2Cr<sup>3+</sup>&nbsp;+&nbsp;Cr<sup>6+</sup>), and the migration of Cr<sup>6+</sup> from the octahedral site in TM layers to the tetrahedral site in sodium layers. Reproduced with permission.<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0030" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0030_R_d148811046e1020" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Chem. Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Copyright 2016, American Chemical Society. b) Calculated energy of Fe migration from the octahedral site in TM layers to the tetrahedral site in Na layers. Reproduced with permission.<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0051" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0051_R_d148811046e1026" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Chem. Mater.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Copyright 2016, American Chemical Society.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In Fe-based layered oxide cathodes, the TM migration is mainly contributed by the Jahn–Teller effect of Fe<sup>4+</sup>O<sub>6</sub>, wherein the high-spin Fe<sup>4+</sup> in distorted Fe<sup>4+</sup>O<sub>6</sub> prefers to migrate from the octahedral sites of TM layers to the Fe<sup>3+</sup>O<sub>4</sub> tetrahedral sites of Na layers with a potential charge transfer between Fe and O during the charge process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0050_R_d148811046e1049" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Theoretical calculation and experiment results provided by Ceder et&nbsp;al. suggested that avoiding the formation of Fe clusters in TM layers could alleviate the Fe<sup>4+</sup>O<sub>6</sub> activity (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202313234-fig-0006">6b</a>) and the migration would be well suppressed when the Fe content was less than 30% in the pristine O3-type layered oxide cathode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0051_R_d148811046e1062" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Chem. Mater.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Substitution is therefore accepted to be the best strategy to suppress the Fe migration. Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>Fe<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0052_R_d148811046e1076" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Nat. Mater.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sub>4</sub>FeRuO<sub>6</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0053_R_d148811046e1086" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Small">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and NaNi<sub>0.3</sub>Co<sub>0.12</sub>Mn<sub>0.18</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>O<sub>2</sub><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0054_R_d148811046e1101" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - J. Phys. Chem. Lett.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> were reported to demonstrate enhanced structure/capacity reversibility. Additionally, Na<sub>0.67</sub>Fe<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.65</sub>Ni<sub>0.15</sub>O<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0055_R_d148811046e1117" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Energy Environ. Sci.">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>0.66</sub>Fe<sub>0.20</sub>Cu<sub>0.14</sub>O<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0056_R_d148811046e1133" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Chem. Mater.">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sub>0.75</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>Fe<sub>0.2</sub>Cu<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0057_R_d148811046e1148" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> were also reported to show excellent structure/capacity reversibility.</p>
         
         <p>Although the two mechanisms proposed above may explain Cr migration and Fe migration, respectively, a general mechanism to explain the origin of TM migration is still required. Very recently, the general origin of TM migration in layered SIB cathodes was first elucidated by our research team.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0058_R_d148811046e1156" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In this study, the TM migration was found to be originated from the formation of Na-free layer when sodium ion was deinserted, and Na-free layer was derived from a random desodiation reaction process. For the random desodiation reaction process, the TM (NaCrO<sub>2</sub> as a model, <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202313234-fig-0007">7a</a>)&nbsp;in two adjacent TM layers would be homogeneously oxidized and then the Na near the oxidized TM would be deinserted from the same Na layer, which would subsequently result in the formation of Na-free layer and TM migration. Generally, the formation of Na-free layers would lead to the dramatic shrinkage of sodium layer spacing (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202313234-fig-0007">7b</a>). Additionally, this mechanism was also applicable to Fe-based layered oxide cathodes. Both theoretical calculations and experiments had been conducted to confirm the universal TM migration, such as density functional theory calculations, atomic STEM, and galvanostatic intermittent titration technique. To avoid the formation of Na-free layers, a selective desodiation reaction process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202313234-fig-0007">7c</a>) was created by a Ru/Ti co-doping strategy. In the Ru/Ti co-doped Na<sub>0.85</sub>Cr<sub>0.85</sub>Ru<sub>0.10</sub>Ti<sub>0.05</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NCRT), the doped active 4<i>d</i> Ru preferentially oxidized over Cr during charge and the doped inactive 3<i>d</i> Ti would function as pillars, resulting in a selective desodiation reaction process. For the crystal structure without the formation of Na-free layer, no dramatic shrinkage of sodium layer spacing appeared even under high desodiation states (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202313234-fig-0007">7d</a>). Additionally, in response to the proposed mechanism and the developed strategy, a high electrochemical reversibility of NCRT (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202313234-fig-0007">7e</a>) was obtained and stable 1000 cycles could be achieved (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202313234-fig-0007">7f</a>). Furthermore, the Ru/Ti co-doping strategy was also found to be adopted to the Fe-based layered oxide cathodes (Na<sub>0.85</sub>Fe<sub>0.85</sub>Ru<sub>0.10</sub>Ti<sub>0.05</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202313234-fig-0007">7g</a>).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202313234-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/cc0392b4-0e40-4315-ab9d-336e97d75955/adfm202313234-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/cc0392b4-0e40-4315-ab9d-336e97d75955/adfm202313234-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d484f9a7-fbeb-421d-9d5a-c5c57cbed562/adfm202313234-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/cc0392b4-0e40-4315-ab9d-336e97d75955/adfm202313234-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202313234-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202313234" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Atomic structures of NaCrO<sub>2</sub> with different Na vacancies (0, left; 25%, middle; 67%, right). NaCrO<sub>2</sub> shows random desodiation reaction process and prefers to form the Na-free layer. b) Calculated interlayer distances of charged NaCrO<sub>2</sub> with 67% Na vacancies. The yellow ball represents sodium atom, the octahedron represents TMO<sub>6</sub>, and the dotted circle represents sodium vacancy. c) Atomic structures of NCRT with different Na vacancies (0, left; 33%, middle; 67%, right). NCRT shows selective desodiation reaction process and the formation of Na-free layer is suppressed. d) Calculated interlayer distances of charged NCRT with 67% Na vacancies. The yellow ball represents sodium atom, the octahedron represents TMO<sub>6</sub>, and the dotted circle represents sodium vacancy. e) Initial three charge and discharge curves of NaCrO<sub>2</sub> and NCRT at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> in the voltage window of 1.5–3.92&nbsp;V. f) Long cycle life of NCRT at 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> in the voltage window of 1.5–3.92&nbsp;V. g) Comparison of the electrochemical stability of NaFeO<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>0.85</sub>Fe<sub>0.85</sub>Ru<sub>0.10</sub>Ti<sub>0.05</sub>O<sub>2</sub> at 10&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> in the voltage window of 2.0–3.5&nbsp;V. Reproduced with permission.<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0058" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0058_R_d148811046e1266" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Copyright 2023, Wiley-VCH.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In addition to TM, immobile alkali/alkaline-earth metals Ca and Mg were also introduced into sodium layers to suppress the formation of Na-free layers and subsequent TM migration.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0059_R_d148811046e1275" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Energy Storage Mater.">59</a>, <a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0060_R_d148811046e1275" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Immobile Ca introduction was reported by Kim et&nbsp;al., where the divalent Ca was incorporated into the octahedral coordination of Na layers in O3-type Na<sub>0.9</sub>Ca<sub>0.035</sub>Cr<sub>0.97</sub>Ti<sub>0.03</sub>O<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0059_R_d148811046e1291" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Energy Storage Mater.">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Benefiting from the strong interaction of Ca and O in Na layers, the unfavorable phase transitions was inhabited, and the structural stability was also enhanced. Alkaline-earth metal Mg was successfully introduced into Na layers by Zhou et&nbsp;al. in P2-type Na<sub>0.7</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>Ni<sub>0.4</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, where the introduced Mg was located in both Na and TM sites, namely Na<sub>0.7</sub>Mg<sub>0.05</sub>[Mn<sub>0.6</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Mg<sub>0.15</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0060_R_d148811046e1318" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The introduced Mg in Na sites served as a pillar to stabilize the crystal even under high charge potentials.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202313234-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202313234-sec-0070-title">4 Utilizing TM Migration</h2>
         
         <p>Based on the origin of the TM migration mentioned above, first proposed in our previous work, we find that many of the previous strategies to enhance the structural/electrochemical reversibility of layered oxide cathodes are consistent with our finding in suppressing the formation of Na-free layers. The Fe with migration property was pinned in Na layers by our research team to suppress the formation of Na-free layers and subsequent TM migration.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0061_R_d148811046e1332" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">61</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the P2-type Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.5-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>Fe<i><sub>x</sub></i>O<sub>2</sub> system, Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NMC) without Fe showed no TM in sodium layers (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202313234-fig-0008">8a,d</a>), Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.4</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NMCF0.1) had few TMs in sodium layers (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202313234-fig-0008">8b,e</a>), while Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.3</sub>Fe<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NMCF0.2) had a lot of TMs in sodium layers (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202313234-fig-0008">8c,f</a>). Compared with NMC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202313234-fig-0008">8g</a>) without TM in sodium layers, NMCF0.1 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202313234-fig-0008">8h</a>) showed a negligible increase in internal stress, while NMCF0.2 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202313234-fig-0008">8i</a>) showed a dramatic increase in internal stress. Further EDS mapping (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202313234-fig-0008">8j</a>) with atomic-level resolution revealed that the TM in the sodium layers was Fe. NMCF0.1 with appropriate amount of TM in sodium layers achieved high structural/electrochemical reversibility due to the suppression of the formation of Na-free layers during charge. Based on these, atomic STEM, geometric phase analysis (GPA), and EDS with atomic-level resolution were suggested to be effective tools to explore TM migration for designing high-performance layered oxide cathodes. Benefiting from the Fe in sodium layers, the structural stability was well enhanced (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202313234-fig-0008">8k</a>) and the Fe in sodium layers showed no any negative effect on sodium ion diffusion (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202313234-fig-0008">8l</a>), which resulted in the stable cycle stability of NMCF0.1 even over 1000&nbsp;cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202313234-fig-0008">8m</a>) Additionally, Ni was also successfully introduced into the sodium layers of Na<sub>0.8</sub>Ni<sub>0.3</sub>Co<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub> by our research team.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0038_R_d148811046e1427" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Sci. Bull.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Benefiting from the TM in sodium layers, the material showed stable cycle performance and an ultra-low lattice variation in the whole charge/discharge process.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202313234-fig-0008"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/08ef0580-f304-4265-aac4-1c98d28ce315/adfm202313234-fig-0008-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/08ef0580-f304-4265-aac4-1c98d28ce315/adfm202313234-fig-0008-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f999c897-8f07-438f-a8ba-9bfd8ce622d7/adfm202313234-fig-0008-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/08ef0580-f304-4265-aac4-1c98d28ce315/adfm202313234-fig-0008-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 8<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202313234-fig-0008&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202313234" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Atomic STEM pictures of a) NMC, b) NMCF0.1, and c) NMCF0.2. Reproduced with permission.<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adfm202313234-bib-0061" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202313234-bib-0061_R_d148811046e1452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">61</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Copyright 2021, Wiley-VCH.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202313234-sec-0080">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202313234-sec-0080-title">5 Summary and Outlook</h2>
         
         <p>Given that TM migration is a common phenomenon in layered oxide cathodes and usually induce unfavorable structural rearrangement and subsequent Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion blockage, resulting in poor initial efficiency (or poor initial capacity reversibility) and capacity degradation, it is believed that suppressing and utilizing TM migration is an effective way to obtain high-performance cathodes. Up to now, there are three main mechanisms to explain TM migration. The first migration mechanism is proposed in NaCrO<sub>2</sub>, where the formation of Cr<sup>4+</sup>–Cr<sup>4+</sup>–Cr<sup>4+</sup> triplets is the trigger factor. The second mechanism is proposed in NaFeO<sub>2</sub>, where the Jahn–Teller effect of Fe<sup>4+</sup> is the trigger factor. The third migration mechanism is recently proposed by our research team in both NaCrO<sub>2</sub> and NaFeO<sub>2</sub>, where the formation of Na-free layer triggers the TM migration. In addition, the third mechanism can also be adopted to other TM migration in layered oxide cathodes. Based on the first two mechanisms, introducing large amounts of other TM into the Cr/Fe sites can effectively suppress the migration. For the third mechanism, doping small amounts of metals into the TM sites or introducing trace immobile metals into the Na site can well avoid the formation of Na-free layer to suppress the migration.</p>
         
         <p>Although great advances have been made in exploring and suppressing TM migration in SIBs, further efforts are still needed. First, although three TM migration mechanisms have been successfully proposed, the first two mechanisms can only be adopted to NaCrO<sub>2</sub> and NaFeO<sub>2</sub>, respectively, and the third mechanism faces the difficulty of characterizing Na-free layers. Further exploration of TM migration is still necessary. Second, the current suppression strategies are also unsatisfactory because they are all at the expense of capacity. Novel/effective strategies are needed. Third, the mechanism for stabilizing structural stability through utilizing TM migration is not well understood. Further fundamental understanding of utilizing TM migration needs to be developed. Generally, we believe that a thorough understanding of TM migration will gradually eliminate the negative effects and lead to the widespread application of SIBs equipped with layered oxide cathodes in large-scale electrochemical energy storage.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
